0374290024|5.0|Ian Morris demonstrates with much conviction that neither the surmised superiority of Western thought (\\"long-term lock-in\\" theories) nor the temporary, largely accidental leadership of the West after 1800 CE (\\"short-term accident\\" theories) offers a satisfactory explanation of why the West currently rules (pp. 13-14; 17-18; 73; 166-167; 226; 260; 474; 521; 564; 572; 575; 619-620).  As Mr. Morris points out correctly, the partisans of these theories largely ignore thousands of years during which the West did not move along a straight line to its eventual dominance of the world (pp. 169-170).  Famine, disease, migration, state collapse, and climate change played at times a key role in shaping social development, which measures a community's ability to get things done (pp. 144; 560; 598).<br /><br />Mr. Morris explores two key themes in the book under review:<br />1) Although biology and sociology explain the global similarities, geography explains the regional differences;<br />2) While geography decides where development rises or falls fastest, social development also determines what geography means (pp. 30; 592).<br /><br />To make his case, Mr. Morris invites his readers to embark on an epic journey to explore what happened between 14000 BCE and today mainly in Eurasia (pp. 130; 166; 558; 640-641).  The globe's most developed societies have always been ones that found their genesis in either the original Western or the original Eastern core (p. 32).<br /><br />Mr. Morris finds out that the West has ruled the world for the last sixteen millennia with the exception of a major parenthesis between about 550 and 1775 CE when the Eastern regions of Eurasia took over from their Western counterparts (pp. 25; 129-133; 223; 332; 337; 395-396; 422; 429; 476; 564).  The examination of this time range allows Mr. Morris to clearly demonstrate that \\"short-term accident\\" theories of why the West rules for now do not make sense at all. Geography has been the key to driving social development in the favor of the West for most of the last sixteen millennia.  However, geography does not exist in a vacuum.  Social development also defines what geography represents (pp. 30-31; 41; 82; 117-118; 144; 271; 427; 557; 561; 565; 572; 592; 619-620; 643).  Mr. Morris observes that the higher social development rises, the faster it can continue rising.  Each innovation builds on earlier ones and contributes to later ones (pp. 194-195).  To come to these conclusions, Mr. Morris develops an original human development index to measure the evolution of social development during this period.  Urbanism, energy capture, information processing, and capacity to make war are the key metrics that Mr. Morris uses to measure the ups and downs of social development over time (pp. 143-171; 623-640).  The most that any great men and \\"bungling idiots\\" could do until 1945 CE was to speed up or slow down processes that were already under way (pp. 316; 416-417; 429; 449; 479; 565-568; 616).<br /><br />Mr. Morris convincingly shows that the great transformations of social development, i.e., the origins of agriculture, the rise of cities and states, the creation of different kinds of empires, and the industrial revolution were each the result of desperate times calling for desperate measures (pp. 559-560).  Human core societies have repeatedly seen their further social development blocked, what Mr. Morris calls their hard ceilings (pp. 34; 560; 563-565; 598; 607).  Rising social development results into bigger populations, more elaborate lifestyles, and greater wealth and military power (pp. 225; 561).  These changes unleash forces, i.e., famine, disease, migration, and state collapse, which undermine social development, especially if they occur at the time of climate change (p. 560).  At the same time, bigger, more complex societies tend to have more sophisticated ways to respond to the bigger, more threatening disruptions that they generate (pp. 225; 404; 418-420; 533; 598).Mr. Morris calls this seemingly contradictory phenomenon the paradox of development (pp. 28; 34; 225; 329-330; 504; 530; 560-562; 601).  This paradox is the main reason why \\"long-term lock-in\\" theories are unsatisfactory in explaining why the West rules for now (p. 195).<br /><br />Mr. Morris forecasts that the East will catch up again with the West by exploiting the advantages of backwardness.  The East is expected to bypass the West by the beginning of the twenty-second century if their respective human development indexes continue to increase at their current speed (pp. 582-583; 619).  Furthermore, Mr. Morris predicts that in the twenty-first century, social development will go up so high that geography will become meaningless (p. 619).<br /><br />However, Mr. Morris has reservations about his own predictions.  The paradox of development mentioned above could wreak these predictions due to the currently perceived limits in the expected growth rates of urbanism, energy capture, information processing, and capacity to make war (pp. 590-598; 608-613).  In addition, Mr. Morris reminds his audience that geography will remain unfair in the twenty-first century, which will eventually allow the West to keep its lead over the rest of the world (pp. 601-602).  Finally, Mr. Morris reminds his audience that with the continuous rise in (nuclear) military capacity, great men and \\"bungling idiots\\" with access to these weapons have had the ability to change history since 1945 CE.<br /><br />In summary, Mr. Morris hopes that humanity will learn not only from archeology and the media, but also from history to save itself from its unmatched capacity for auto-destruction (pp. 621-622).y contradictory phenomenon the paradox of development (pp. 28; 34; 225; 329-330; 504; 530; 560-562; 601).  This paradox is the main reason why \\"long-term lock-in\\" theories are unsatisfactory in explaining why the West rules for now (p. 195). The East is expected to bypass the West by the beginning of the twenty-second century if their respective human development indexes continue to increase at their current speed (pp. 582-583; 619).  Furthermore, Mr. Morris predicts that in the twenty-first century, social development will go up so high that geography will become meaningless (p. 619).      However, Mr. Morris has reservations about his own predictions.  The paradox of development mentioned above could wreak these predictions due to the currently perceived limits in the expected growth rates of urbanism, energy capture, information processing, and capacity to make war (pp. 590-598; 608-613). B0043URV8M|4.0|Most movie franchises fall flat well before their seventh outing. The Harry Potter series has not, probably for two main reasons: there's always been some sense (even before all the books were released) of where the franchise was headed, and there's been continued participation of key cast and crew members (including the main trio of the series, most of the adult cast, producer David Heyman, and writer Steve Kloves). So it's easy to see why the scores for the Harry Potter movies have quavered in quality much more than the movies for which they were written: they have no sense of direction and there have now been four composers for the last five films.    Desplat is no different than Doyle and Hooper in the way he treats Williams's original themes for the first few movies. Like those two before him, Desplat takes the the first few bars of Williams's \\"Hedwig's Theme\\", inserts it into a few tracks, and  ignores the rest of Williams's material. At this point, it shouldn't be expected to hear a reprisal of Williams themes like Diagon Alley, Quidditch, or Window to the Past. Those themes were written topically and it's doubtful even Williams himself would have found it important to use them (he abandoned most of his own themes in his score for Prisoner of Azkaban, after all). The least Desplat could have done, however, was made a full reference to Hedwig's Theme, as it really is the iconic musical track of the series. Particularly noticeable is the theme's absence from the album's opening tracks--it's first fragmented appearance is in the third track of the album, \\"Polyjuice Potion\\" . Hopefully, the theme will be played at the beginning of the score in the movie itself, as it has been with each of the last six movies.    But there are more important things. Taking up a scoring assignment so late in the series makes the lack of direction in the series more conspicuous for Desplat than it was for either Doyle or Hooper. Ultimately, though, Desplat didn't do Williams any less justice than his two predecessors, and the music he did write is pretty impressive.     The main theme of the 2009 fantasy movie he scored, \\"The Twilight Saga: New Moon\\" was the titular track \\"New Moon.\\" The opening track of this Deathly Hallows score, \\"The Oblivation\\", blows \\"New Moon\\" away in its soaring melodic phrases and more interesting orchestral development. The two tracks are actually constructed very similarly, a fairly simple theme being played over a low string ostinato and gradually developed. The Oblivation is not the main theme of the soundtrack (although it introduces one of the major new themes), but seems to me rather more like an overture to the score.    Arguably the major new theme of the soundtrack is introduced at the beginning of \\"Polyjuice Potion\\". Based on a three-note octave figure, it's played first by violins and horn and is quickly taken over in a minor form by trumpet and then English horn. It sounds almost pastoral, and easily could represent Harry's feelings towards the Burrow (which is the Weasley's place of residence, for all of you non-Potterheads). Instead, Desplast seems to contort the theme throughout the film to match changing moods, for example at 2:56 in \\"Sky Battle\\". Most characteristically, he arranges a tragic version of the theme for piano in \\"Harry and Ginny\\", appropriately conveying Harry and Ginny's longing to be together but the necessity that they stay apart. This approach works, but I almost wish Depslat had taken inspiration from the \\"Romeo and Juliet\\" track from the New Moon soundtrack. A proper theme for their conflicted relationship would have been most effective.     Finally, something I really like--a theme for the Death Eaters. The series has needed one ever since Goblet of Fire when they were first introduced, but Doyle and Hooper both failed to write one. First introduced in \\"Snape to Malfoy Manor\\" and then reprised in \\"Death Eaters\\", the theme adequately portrays the Death Eaters and should play well inthe film.    A third theme is a very simple one first introduced in \\"The Oblivation\\" and then played at the outset of \\"Death Eaters\\", by solo cello in \\"Ron Leaves\\", and again by high violins in \\"Ron's Speech\\". Due to this random assortment I can't tell what the theme is supposed to represent exactly, but it's perhaps the best-developed identifiable theme on the album.    After having nothing but Hooper Harry Potter scores for the last 5 years, listening to this soundtrack is like taking a first breath after minutes of holding it in. Finally, a full brass section is heard in all its glory. There's satisfyingly complex action music, interesting modulations, and a rich palette of orchestral colors.  This album earns a better rating than either of Hooper's with its opening track alone.     Let's instead compare this soundtrack to those of Williams and Doyle. The music Williams established for the franchise is highly effective and, even six soundtracks later, could still inspire new music for the franchise. Desplat's approach to the score is somewhat similar to Williams. There are no concert suites to be found on the album but the way he develops cues for individual scenes is very like Williams. \\"Sky Battle\\" recalls \\"Quidditch, Third Year\\" and \\"Destroying the Locket\\" recalls \\"Dementors Converge\\". He handles the cues for Dobby and Xenophilius Lovegood like how you would expect Williams to (although the actual theme Williams wrote for Dobby is not heard here).     You can just tell the score will be effective in the film from listening to the soundtrack. It's too competently made for it not to. But, although the music is effective, and Desplat certainly earned his paycheck, it could seldom be described as magical. If you listen to Williams's scores, he infuses magical wonder into nearly every cue, from the iconic \\"Hedwig's Theme\\" to the breathtaking \\"Buckbeak's Flight\\" to the darkest moments of his scores, like in \\"The Face of Voldemort.\\" All of his cues simply sound likeHarry Potter. A lot of the music on this DH1 soundtrack sounds like it could have been interchanged with cues from \\"The Last Airbender\\".     As for Doyle--like Desplat, he ignored Williams's music except for a few uses of Hedwig's Theme, but the music he wrote to replace Williams's had a strong sense of identity and moreover had an incredible sense of storytelling. You can see the movie play in your head when you listen to the track \\"The Goblet of Fire\\" or \\"Harry Sees Dragons.\\" Desplat's soundtrack in contrast starts to lag during the middle and many minutes of music accumulate that contribute nothing to the score overall.     There are standout moments that rank with the best of Harry Potter music, including the two Ron tracks, \\"Hermione's Parents\\", \\"The Elder Wand\\", and, my personal favorite, \\"Fireplaces Escape.\\" But then there are tracks like \\"Godric's Hollow Graveyard\\" and \\"Bathilda Bagshot\\" that are mostly written according to strict conventions. Since Williams and Doyle both wrote homerun soundtracks, it's only fair to expect the absolute best from Desplat. Instead, he's given us good, and occasionally very good.     John Williams started off the franchise as a composer with already 42 years of experience and 5 Oscars. Desplat is only 49 years of age himself. It is no longer the expectation that Williams's themes be developed for the late movies in the franchise; if Doyle didn't and Hooper didn't, it's certainly not Desplat's duty to. But, frankly, even when Williams abandoned most of his own themes for his score in Prisoner of Azkaban, his vast experience allowed him to write beautiful music for the film that worked perfectly as a score and on soundtrack. Maybe Alexandre Desplat will be able to write music like that when he's 70. For now, though, it's possible that no Harry Potter score can truly live up to Williams's first three, simply because they were not written by Williams. The piano music in \\"Harry and Ginny\\" is a first for the franchise. Desplat really stuck to his style for this score, and the resulting music is probably the best fantasy score he's done (surpassing \\"The Golden Compass\\" and \\"New Moon\\"). While he isn't Williams, this music was written with conviction, and it shows.    I'd probably rank this soundtrack above the two Hooper scores, below Sorcerer's Stone, Prisoner of Azkaban, and Goblet of Fire, and perhaps tied with Chamber of Secrets. Desplast was recently announced to be the composer for Deathly Hallows: part 2, and it'll be interesting to see where he takes the music for the finale. That project will demand grand action music, dark prose, touching character moments, and something poignant to wrap everything up. I'm just glad he's the one composing and I'm the one reviewing.    I'll be updating this review with my thoughts on how the score plays in the movie, as well as the 6 bonus tracks that were not included in the album.

0316098337|4.0|Donoghue's Room is certainly timely, if often uncomfortably eerie in its explorations of life in confinement and adjustment to the outside world salted with unexpected dangers. A young woman has been confined for seven years by her wily kidnapper in a securely locked 11' x 11' room. At only nineteen, it is impossible to comprehend the psychological impact of her imprisonment, perhaps the only saving grace the birth of her son, Jack, who is now five and the narrative voice of the novel. Donoghue, of course, is brilliant, whether writing historical fiction or contemporary novels, her perspective skewed to the idiosyncrasies that usually go unnoticed in human behavior. But in Room, everything is important, from Bed, where Ma and Jack sleep, to Rug, stained by Jack's entry into Room and his deliverance from it, even Tooth, a remnant of Ma's painful decay that Jack carries as a totem into the next phase of his life.<br /><br />The implications of such confinement are horrific, but Donoghue forces us into intimacy with circumstances beyond the shock value of the crime, the day to day challenges of a symbiotic relationship between mother and son, Jack unable to comprehend the separateness of Ma-and-Me. A tale of isolation becomes one of accommodation to \\"Outside\\" and the many obstacles awaiting a boy who has defined himself in relation to a tiny room, where inanimate objects are friends and the television images of people are fantasy. Accommodation to Outside is painful and shocking, a dog's bite, navigating stairs for the first time, orange juice with pulp (germs) and people who have no capacity for the infinite patience required to match child to a new reality. The author plumbs the dark places, Ma's unerring instinct to protect her son from \\"Old Nick\\", her own fragile mental state and the love of a mother for the child who saves her from despair.<br /><br />The healing of such a travesty is arduous work, the public easily distracted by the next breaking drama long before mother and child have begun to adjust to circumstances and the myriad times Jack is vulnerable to scrutiny. At times I felt as trapped as Ma, inside or outside Room, exhausted by Jack's litany of questions and his need for assurance. This is Donoghue's genius: to make her reader complicit in the demands and context of an emotional rollercoaster. 0812548094|3.0|This is, unfortunately, a typical sophmore kind of effort from a new writer.  Wizard's First Rule was clearly a labor of love that went through countless edits before it finally found its way into book stores.  Thanks to its success, the sequel likely had very little editing, and was written in a much shorter period of time - and it shows.    I am guessing that the writing process went something like this: The author drafted a roughly 200 page outline of the story, then went back to the beginning and started adding detail.  A LOT of detail.  The first 75 pages of his outline swelled to about 500 pages.  Some of the detail needed adding, but there are also some 5-page-long speeches in there that could and should have been whittled down to a page at most.  My guess is that his editor gave him a free hand, and maybe was looking to do a lot of the editing once the book was complete.  This gives the first half of the book a plodding feel.  Goodkind's determination to make all his villains as wicked as possible doesn't help the mood, either.  Some of the extra detail in the book is used to detail graphically the gruesome aftermath of the sack of a city, and a dark ceremony with lovingly detailed bestiality - I see there's some concern on this site that that particular scene is not fit for teens.  My opinion is that it's not fit for adults either.  I wish there was a way to scrub that scene from my mind.      Suddenly, at about page 500, there is a shift in the level of detail, and the author spills all the secrets that he has lovingly saved up to surprise us with in one big gush.  You can almost feel the book deadline looming as his writing becomes sparse and frantic, with only occasional details, and little character development.  The last 75 pages of the book are probably taken verbatim from his original outline, with no further editing or additions.  The climax is ridiculously easy for our hero, who conveniently has the kind of magic that you don't actually need to learn how to use because it works instinctively.  So, with the world hanging in the balance 50 pages from the end, and several insurmaoutable problems, he basically waves his hand and the problems all go away.  It is a ridiculously simple and unsatisfying ending to a very dark and troubled book.  The worst is the way he is reconciled to Kahlan by a coincidental prayer to the \\"good spirits\\" at the end, which conveniently also solves Adie's problem as well.      The book, overall, is a disappointment, given the quality of Goodkind's first effort. It's clearly hampered by his newfound success, and subsequent deadline.  Goodkind needed to take the time to expand the whole of his outline, and then edit it back down to a reasonable level of detail - instead, we get a book that is too verbose at the start and too terse at the finish, with no happy medium in between.

B00428ZRFY|3.0|`Quiet' would have been a far more appropriate title, since aside from the highly misleading first single, Rihanna's fifth album is filled with ballads and mid-tempo jams. This is not the first time in recent years that this type of bizarre irony has appeared in the music industry; Pink's last album also had a raucous lead single and boisterous title, neither of which had anything to do with the slow, somber remainder of its tracklist.     It's confusing why artists are suddenly choosing to promote and title their album based solely on the lead single, particularly when the single is loudly divergent from past work. It generally gets fans hyped up for a specific sort of theme or style, so needless to say certain expectations can be built up. `Loud' fails to meet any expectations fans likely had after hearing the extremely upbeat electro-pop of single \\"Only Girl (In The World)\\" and leaked track \\"Who's That Chick,\\" the latter of which is tragically absent on the album. In fact, `Loud' doesn't differ spectacularly from album four, `Rated R,' although ironically it has even fewer upbeat songs, and is expectedly less moody in tone.     Sex seems to be the elephant in the `Loud' room, with several of the 11 tracks being dedicated fully or partially to the act and its many variants. \\"Disturbia\\" retread \\"S&M\\" finds Rihanna snarling \\"I may be bad, but I'm perfectly good at it, sex in the air, I don't care, I like the smell of it\\" as she celebrates the joys of sadomasochism, while the slow-burner \\"Skin\\" pretty much speaks for itself. Second single \\"What's My Name?,\\" produced by Stargate and also about sex, features Drake and mostly serves its purpose with its syrupy smooth chorus and honeyed vocal come-ons; it's one of the album's few major standouts.     \\"Cheers (Drink To That)\\" makes unexpected use of an Avril Lavigne sample to decent effect; in spite of its lyrics being celebratory of partying and the weekend, however, the song isn't particularly upbeat and is much more ofa slow-groove jam than its title and topic would suggest. Things slow down even more with \\"Fading,\\" arguably one of the most successful ballads here, which is shockingly reminiscent of an Enya track at its beginning.     By the time \\"Only Girl (In The World)\\" makes its way into the tracklisting it sounds jarringly out of place, sandwiched between two ballads and the only song of its type on the entire album. It also happens to be, by far, the brightest and most exciting offering on `Loud,' and leaves listeners wondering what could have been if Rihanna had dug at least a little deeper into these hard dance beats and soaring vocals. The track that follows, \\"California King Bed\\" is, in spite of its strong vocals, limp, mopey and a total buzzkill.      \\"Man Down,\\" a wonky reggae romp that makes use of very \\"Unfaithful\\"-ish metaphors, isn't much better, but finds Rihanna heavily rediscovering her Bajan accent. \\"Raining Men\\" is extremely typical hip-hop fare featuring Nicki Minaj and continues the album's steady decline in quality. Thankfully things perk up a bit with \\"Complicated,\\" an emotive power ballad with a unique mid-tempo electronic beat.     \\"Love The Way You Lie (Part II)\\" may or may not have been entirely necessary, considering how overwhelmingly omnipresent the original song was all summer, but it retains its strong hook and appeal in this new version, although it doesn't particularly outdo the original. It also further muddies the assumed theme and message of the original, which will undeniably and needlessly fan the controversy's flames even further. On an off-note, however, it is a nice alternative for non-rap fans who enjoyed the original song's chorus, particularly the piano version featuring just Rihanna, which is a bonus track on certain editions.     `Loud' is definitely not what it could have been or was expected to be, offering a dishearteningly small number of strong additions to the chanteuse's catalogue. It may, however, be a necessary placeholder between the moody darkness of `Rated R' and the Rihanna of the future-hopefully we haven't seen the last of that supremely genuine smile and upbeat tone of the \\"Who's That Chick\\" video.

B0040JHXTI|5.0|Darkness On The Edge Of Town was Bruce Springsteen's \\"comeback\\" record.  Put into exile for almost three years after his leap into superstardom with Born To Run over legal hassles, Mr. Springsteen emerged with what he refers to in to the deluxe editions notes as his samurai album.  Feeling the influences of the punk scene, he dispatched with Wall of Sound inspired production of Born To Run and went for a more leaner sonic landscape. In the original album, Mr. Springsteen's characters are no longer looking to escape their problems, they've giving up escape a long time ago. They are searching for answers to the situations they find themselves in. Optimism has been replaced by despair and cynism. \\"Badlands\\" is an angry anthem about never being satisfied with where you are in life. \\"Adam Raised A Cain\\" is about the struggles between a father and his angry son and \\"Factory\\" is about the drudgery of everyday life. Songs like \\"Something In The Night\\", \\"The Promised Land\\" and \\"Racing In The Streets\\" are about chasing a dream that one will probably never find. \\"Candy's Room\\", \\"Streets Of Fire\\" & \\"Prove It All Night\\" are about putting everything on the line for someone and having to constantly prove their devotion. The title song best sums up the album. People are looking for answers to the same questions, willing to pay the cost, but never find them. No matter how much time they search into the Darkness On The Edge Of Town, they end up in the same place.  The remastered version of the album cleans up the sound of the original cd and allows you to experience a more richer sound.  The unreleased tracks (also being issued separately as The Promise) show what a prolific and diverse songwriter he really is.  As he notes in the excellent documentary included here, many of these songs were left off because they didn't fit the mood of the album that he was trying to create.  It's amazing that an artist struggling to remain relevant at that point in his career would leave off sure fire hits like \\"Because The Night\\", \\"Fire\\" & \\"Rendezvous\\" to keep the integrity of his vision.  That type of move would almost be unheard in this day and age.  The three dvd's are worth multiple viewing especially the stellar bootleg concert from 1978.  This set is a definite luxury for any Bruce fan and not really for the casual one.re hits like \\"Because The Night\\", \\"Fire\\" & \\"Rendezvous\\" to keep the integrity of his vision. The unfortunate reality is often content fluff that adds nothing more than entertainment or reads like medical transcriptions.<br /><br />Content loaded with fluff leaves me feeling as if I have learned the subject's core but have been stretched thin on fully understanding anything outside the core.<br /><br />Imagine a doll made of rubber.  Content loaded with fluff expands the doll, but it is much like stretching its limbs instead of building the doll into a well rounded robust figure. There is no set of rules stating writing has to be boring, dull, or condensed soup.  This guide provides a good set of principles that allow you to convey your meaning and sound human.<br /><br />Respect your readers.  Write concisely with a great degree of accuracy and have fun with it.  Get this book and learn how you can deliver all of these things and be loved for it.

0765326353|3.0|The Way of Kings is both the introduction to a new world, the world of the series that Tor is doing everything they can to push forward as the next epic fantasy flagship, and the story of several men who are at the center of the rising storm. At the first of those, The Way of Kings succeeds admirably. What it fails to do, however, is to also excel in the tale that it tells; everything about The Way of Kings is a prologue, and that feeling is hard to ever lose.    Mind you, The Way of Kings does display quite a few of Sanderson's great strengths. Though I would not yet place Sanderson with masters like VanderMeer or Martin when it comes to either the feel or detail of his world, Sanderson's skills as a world builder are exemplary. Roshar, like his other creations, is not your generic medieval world, and what is so enjoyable about Sanderson's settings is that he actually tries to think through the implications of his decisions and then implements the results into every aspect of the book, from plot twists to diction.     At times, this can lead to a slight awkwardness - such as the curse Storm you! that is used a thousand times in the text and, while making perfect sense for the world, feels simply odd to read - the vast majority of Sanderson's decisions in the area are a great success. His ability to draw you into the world and then twist you about once you are there leads to several of his greatest moments, twists that are (in his own words, from Writing Excuses) surprising yet inevitable within the framework that he has constructed.    To give a small example, a king is killed in the prologue, and the assassin who kills him hears his dying words. The assassin regrets the killing and wants to pass the words on, so he writes them down next to the king's corpse. This makes perfect sense to both to the reader and the assassin, coming from outside the Alethi culture that the king is a member of. Alethi men, however, are not meant to be able to read, a fact that is brought up in separate circumstances several times. Later, a character brings up the puzzle of how and why the king learned to read.    So Sanderson's world building is great, and it's very enjoyable when he shows it off, especially in the one off interlude viewpoints that are scattered around the map. Unfortunately, the world building's integration into the main storyline is far from flawless. There are several info dumps within the narrative, and, though they are rarely long enough to be a problem on their own, they occasionally clutter action scenes or hinder the pacing as a whole:     \\"[Szeth] hit the ground in the midst of the soldiers. Completely surrounded, but holding a Shardblade.    According to legend, the Shardblades were first carried by the Knights Radiant uncounted ages ago. Gifts of their god, granted to allow them to fight horrors of rock and flame, dozens of feet tall, foes whose eyes burned with hatred. The voidbringers. When your foe had skin as hard as stone itself, steel was useless. Something supernatural was required.    Szeth rose from his crouch, loose white clothes rippling, jaw clenched against his sins...\\" (p. 28)    Sanderson is known for his magic systems and, in that respect, The Way of Kings does not disappoint. Though the magic is not as front and center as it is in, say, Mistborn, its impact is felt throughout the world and throughout the story. The quest for shards and plates - both of which, at first, appeared to me as something like Masterchief's armor and a Covenant Energy Sword, but I got over that impression soon enough - is both convincing and interesting, and Sanderson ingrains us with enough of the culture's morals and desires that when the relics come into question towards the end of the book the reader can feel the weight of the decision alongside the characters.     Sanderson's prose in The Way of Kings is nothing exemplary, but it's not trying to be, the ultimate goal more in line with a camera lense than a stained glass window. The action scenes are clear, flowing, and cinematic, and the atmosphere comes through strong at several moments, many of them when the highstorms are at their height and dust is swirling just past the windows. At the same time, Sanderson will occasionally repeat himself, leading to certain worldbuilding aspects (spren come to mind, in particular) being reiterated in almost the same words time and time again.     Of the three characters, Kaladin is the one with the most screen time. His story is well told and enjoyable, but it's crippled by the simple fact that every single beat of it is obvious from before the word go. Kaladin is the reluctant hero to the last detail, the man with newfound powers who's afraid of the responsibility they bring, the just and good leader faced with the deaths of his men. It's all well written, and the bridge scenes that pepper his narrative are excellent, but it's hard to get too wrapped up in the tale when every obstacle is so obviously only the most minor of setbacks.     Sanderson has said that each book in The Stormlight Archives will have one \\"main\\" character who will have flashbacks to their past throughout the book, and Kaladin's the man with the past here. The flashbacks are, like much of Way of Kings, interesting but ultimately inconsequential. There are a few very good scenes in his childhood, don't get me wrong, but none of it is unexpected, certainly not the kind of thing that you'd be lost around if you hadn't been given a blow by blow tour beforehand. So, in the end, it's something that's not unenjoyable but also not necessary - fine on its own, but, when placed in a book so filled with excess, one of the traits that conspire to slow the glorious march of Sanderson's world to a more stately crawl.    Dalinar is, like Kaladin, a familiar character. He is the noble lord, the man who will do what is right even if it kills him. What sets Dalinar apart from his fellow Noble Lords is the visions that take him with every highstorm, throwing him into a past place and time and instructing him of what to do in the present. Dalinar acts on what they tell him, but can never get over the fear that he is mad for doing so. Compounding the problem is his son, Alodin, who is convinced that their father is running their house into the ground. The tension between the two is the highlight of the arc, though some of the drama is dampened by the sheer amount of time spent on it. The dilemma is interesting, but Dalinar spends what feels like entire novels worth of text debating his sanity, flip flopping back and forth and announcing grand decisions only to renege on them, then perhaps renege on the reneging, then perhaps... well, you get the idea. Furthermore, the visions are never really resolved, they simply grow in scale, leaving us to try and guess their meaning and importance until the next thousand page opus.    The final main character is Shallan, and her chapters hold some of my favorite and least favorite aspects of The Way of Kings as a whole. Shallan is attempting to become the ward of world renowned scholar Jasnah, the king's sister, and, as such, her chapters involve more studying and contemplation than slashing power swords through breastplates. The change of pace helps the story keep from growing stale, and Shallan's character comes through perhaps the best of the three. Her storyline lacks the major upheavals of, say, Dalinar's, but the information uncovered is interesting and the relationships that develop feel natural and conflicted. Best of all are Sanderson's descriptions of her art, which colors her chapters beautifully and makes her immensely likeable, at least from my perspective.     Of course, it's also interesting to see that the person facing the far, far more minor decisions than our beloved Noble Lord also makes her decision in a bare fraction of the page's that he does - perhaps a novella rather than his seven volume saga. Still, a novella's worth of buildup is hardly rapid, and how much you enjoy Shallan's plotline will, in large part, depend on how much you are willing to tolerate character's debating over imaginary topics. Those sections are broken up by Shallan's wit, which is, to put it lightly, a mixed bag. Most of what she says is decently clever, but it's the kind of clever where you smirk for about a second, not the kind where everyone in the room starts guffawing - which, of course, everyone does. Every time. That's not to imply that there's nothing of real interest going on, though; Shallan's plotline has one of the novel's absolute coolest scenes about halfway through it, and the slow build leads to an excellent climax.     The final recurring viewpoint character appears only in interludes and shadows, which is a shame because it's in Szeth's storyline that Sanderson really plays to his strengths. Szeth's is a fast moving tale filled with obscured meanings and motivations, all made great by clever action and an ability to think through consequences in a way both understandable and unpredictable:    \\"Szeth-son-son-Vallano, Truthless of Shinovar, wore white on the day he was to kill a king. The white clothing was a Parshendi tradition, foreign to him. but he did as his masters required and did not ask for an explanation.\\" (p. 21)    Shallan's climax isn't the only great one. It's only at the end of the book that Sanderson really steps it up, but, when he does, it's impossible to miss the fireworks. The Way of Kings ends with a barrage of confrontations and twists that propelled everything that came before to new heights, validated some of the long waits, didn't quite validate some others, and was generally a more subdued version of the explosion of twists at the end of Mistborn, with the difference here being that the foundation Sanderson was building off of was even greater.     It's probably telling, though, that I said, for all three major plotlines, that the buildup was too long. It is, for each and every one of them.There are no truly wasted scenes in The Way of Kings, no six chapter sub plot that feels like a pure digression upon completion, but there is still far, far, far too much stuff hanging out in that gray area where, yes, it does give us a bit more insight to the character, but it takes up twenty pages, and when you do it again and again for this guy, and again and again for that guy, you've got a book that doesn't so much strut as stagger. The set pieces are all good - great, even - and the characters are generally good, the atmosphere's engrossing, all of that's where it should be, but there's simply too much book here. The world building was needed to establish the epic feel that Sanderson was going for, and it's the best part of the book, but it's hard to deny that the world building got tired, sat down, and smothered the main plot under its many, many, gratuitous layers of fat.     In short, I'm not saying that Way of Kings is a bad book. It's not. I'm not saying that Way of Kings is the best series opener since A Game of Thrones, because it's not. What it is, is a good, enjoyable book that's got some flaws, but also has plenty to love for fans of Epic Fantasy. Will Sanderson make The Stormlight Archive more than that? I suppose we'll have to wait and see.

B003UERZ88|5.0|The Metal God returns with the latest album from his self named solo band entitled Made Of Metal (cracker of a name that too!).  Here is another album that I was really looking forward to getting my hands on this year, albeit with some degree of reservation after what I consider to be a pretty average album in his last (metal) one Crucible.  Whilst that album did have some tracks on it that I like I feel that it fell far short of the standard normally expected from Halford.  It went in a direction that I was not pleased about at all with its chugging, plodding, heavier but ultimately boring riffs and screaming just for the sake of sounding aggressive rather than to vocally enhance the songs.  Early snippets of Made Of Metal that I got from Youtube and poor Amazon reviews did little to inspire me to rush out and get this album either.  Plus with the title song Made Of Metal having lyrics that made even me question its credibility - Nascar racing?  What is he thinking? Then the second song I heard entitled The Mower had the persistent screams, heaviness and chugging nature typical of stuff from Crucible, so I wasn't expecting much from the album at all, but being a Halford album I still bought it.<br /><br />I still had some reservations too upon hearing the lyrics of the opening track Undisputed with its boxing theme - what's with another song about sport?  But I soon found what a pleasant surprise this album has turned out to be!  This album will no doubt be somewhat of a disappointment for anyone looking for another Crucible,  those who want nothing but heaviness, speed and aggression from Halford.  But for the likes of me who grew up with Judas Priest in the 1980's this album is a welcome return to the kind of music that I loved so much but feared I would never hear again from Judas Priest or Halford.  Simply put it is heaven to my ears.<br />There is a mixed bag of styles on this album with the songs sounding like they could have fitted on The Halford album Resurrection (which I love) and also several Priest albums from 1978's Stained Class to 1988's Ram It Down.  However what these songs all have in common is what was at the core of Priest's appeal to me over two decades ago now. i.e. hard music, a great rhythm and beat, one of THE best vocalists ever, but underpinning it all was the melody.  Priest seemed to be one of the best at perfectly capturing the right balance of that mix, and Halford has done it again here.<br /><br />Sure this is not as heavy as a lot of music today, and it doesn't have the fast complex riffs, but music like this was considered heavy 25 years ago it still sounds tough enough for me today.  There are some simple but great riffs on offer here and good solos which are done to enhance the song rather than show how clever the guitarists are.  In essence it still packs a fair punch.<br /><br />Production on this album is pretty much flawless, much better than the mushed up wall of noise that was Crucible.  Each instrument is clean and easily distinguishable in the mix, but most importantly Halford himself is right out there so you can hear him clearly.  So often I gripe about the vocalist being too far back in the mix these days, but not here.  This is heavy metal music produced as it should be.  So hats off to Roy Z.<br /><br />With an album like this I could go on about individual songs, how good they are and why I like them, but I've gone on enough already so I'll just say that despite the sporting references Undisputed has grown on me so I now like it a lot, and so has the title track to the degree that I now rate it an absolute killer.  Then there's Speed Of Sound, the catchy We Own The Night, Hell Razor, Twenty-Five Years, Matador, and my ultimate favorite from this album Heartless.  Even the others not mentioned are by no means bad songs, I like them a lot too.  But the closer The Mower still remains the least appealing to me.  I'm a huge fan of Halfords high-pitched vocals, the likes of which sound great on Priest songs such as Victim Of Changes, The Sentinel, Devils Child, Painkiller and many more, but he overdoes it on The Mower and it sounds forced.  The album could have done without The Mower, but it is only one song, so this is still an album that really does it for me.Victim Of Changes, The Sentinel, Devils Child, Painkiller and many more, but he overdoes it on The Mower and it sounds forced.  The album could have done without The Mower, but it is only one song, so this is still an album that really does it for me.

Josh is such a clown in his spoken life, and sometimes he wonders why people think he is so serious, but the music sounds very serious, almost grim.  --------------------  Second Addendum  In his \\"Before We Begin\\" concert at Union Chapel, Josh explained that \\"London Hymn\\" was written in London, shortly after he wrote \\"The War at Home.\\"  It is a sort of quiet requiem piece that has afterthoughts about his visit to Walter Reed Hospital.  -----------------  Third addendum  In his \\"Before We Begin\\" concert in Toronto, Jan. 13, Josh explained that \\"Au jardin des sans pourquoi\\" had lyrics written by Rufus Wainwright and his mother, both of whom are or were noted songwriters. though Josh wrote the music .  At one of the \\"Before We Begin\\" concerts, Wainright came up to ask if the song had made it onto the record.  It turned out it was the only song he had written with his mother and she had died shortly thereafter.  ----------------  Fourth addendum:  I saw a recent interview where Josh said that \\"If I Walk Away\\" was written to his parents and other people in his life who keep him grounded -- to encourage them to continue keeping him grounded.

B0044XV3QY|2.0|*** Be advised, Abrahamic themes discussed here ***    Technicals aside, Avatar appears to be Cameron's take on how the biblical Book of Joshua might play out allegorically but reversed and depicted as science fiction. Imagine a fantasy where the alien, \\"pagan\\" enemies of some futuristic, space-faring \\"Israel\\" instead have the righteousness and might of the \\"true\\" deity on their side. In his cinematic brilliance, Cameron shows just how this futuristic, figurative \\"Israel\\" is brought to its knees by its would-be alien (figurative biblical pagan) targets. The humans are snarkily depicted as claiming the superior means and values, yet in this fantasy they are THE god-forsaken, rapacious foreigners - in the eyes of the pagans.    The blue Na'vi in the movie appear to represent a fantastic version of the ancient, partly-human Anakite giants (Anakite: long-neck) of the biblical land of Canaan, metaphorically represented here by Pandora. Since the movie is from the human traitor Jake Sully's (alternate biblical Caleb) and his alien allies' POV (Sully: \\"Excuse me, this is my video log here!\\"), there is of course no attribution to any kind of divine hand behind this metaphorical Israelite army. OTOH, it is a force which does possess a thematic proxy for the power of the human (biblical Israel's) deity, in the form of unimaginable - in the eyes of the pagans - advantages in armaments, high technology, and organization. Not coincidentally, the human (biblical Israeli) army is depicted transiting the wilderness of space from a blighted Earth (biblical Egypt) while subsisting on in-flight rations (biblical manna), bent on an overarching mission to seek their nation's survival and prosperity by winning the rights to the promised rock (biblical land) of Pandora (biblical Canaan). The idea of \\"Israel\\" as the divinely favored people is figured in the movie as a powerful human civilization aspiring to \\"Unobtanium\\", a \\"Rock\\" no less, which in the end proves to be unobtainable due to alien enemy action. Which is precisely Cameron's point, and likely motivation.    After being forced to take a swim (biblical Jordan) by the nasty wildlife (biblical \\"land swallows up people and things\\"), ex-Marine (Israelite warrior) Jake meets Na'vi princess Neytiri, a figurative stand-in for the ravishingly beautiful biblical Rahab. In this tale, Rahab's biblical character as fearful prostitute is uplifted into a stalwart and loyal Na'vi (biblical Canaanite) princess. Rahab's biblical role as the Israeli sympathizer who hid the two Israeli spies scouting Jericho is smartly reversed into that of a die-hard noble pagan defender and seducer. This version of Rahab also plays a secondary role as that infamous Moabite princess who carried out the orders of biblical Canaanite king Balak (paralleling the biblical advice of the pagan sorcerer Balaam not to confront Israel indirectly) to seduce an enemy Israeli warrior into betraying his own nation. Instead of getting speared in the act with said enemy (biblical Phinehas/Zimri incident) as in the original story, this revised Rahab-cum-sex-princess does get to turn the tables by spearing the enemy Colonel, who appears to stand for biblical Joshua due to his age and character (Yes, yes, I know that the original production code-name for the Jake Sully character is \\"Joshua\\"). The Neytiri (biblical Rahab) character ends up as a fantastic re-imagining of the ancient pagan war-goddess Neith/Anat - she of nakedly aggressive beauty and skill with bow and arrow. A sneaky reversal of an Old Testament story marvelously retold from the Canaanite point of view.     Among other things, Pandora/Canaan's magical floating mountains are metaphors for those ancient mountaintops from where biblical King Balak and Sorcerer Balaam tried to hex their Israelite enemies. It's a bit of a thematic stretch, but these can also represent the high places where Israelis would not only practice worship, but more importantly also seal holy covenants, or marriages between entities. On the other hand, the offsite Avatar link couch assigned to Sully is revealingly named \\"Beulah\\" (Dr. Augustine: \\"You'll be in the link at the end. Unit one. Beulah...\\"), which means \\"Israel\\" and/or \\"married\\" in Hebrew. Hebrew prophets were always warning Israelites against marrying irredeemable aliens. The prophets particularly condemned syncretic corruption involving divinity marriages between the Hebrew God YHWH and pagan goddesses such as Asherah, whose domain was trees and was symbolized by the presence of wooden poles, carved wooden figures, and trees. Of which stuff the figurative pagans have more than a little of. So in this reversal, an \\"Israelite\\" turns against his nation and people when he marries into a rival enemy \\"alien religion\\". Cameron must have been chortling as he thought this one up.    The age of the elder chief scientist Dr. Augustine and and her expertise in botany marks her character as figurative of the first failed biblical spy team which was tasked by Moses to scout biblical Canaan's agriculture. Sully is buddied in a paired spy-team just as in the original story. We have a fantastical re-creation of the biblical Canaanite mother goddess Asherah, associated with the living tree of fertility and child-sacrifice rites and enemy-god to the biblical Israelites. Not to mention sex in the pagan commons under a tree - are no private quarters available to the aliens? The merging-of-braids-and-minds depicted in the Collector's edition follows the sex-religion aspects of the ancient Asherah pagan cult, which saw public sexual union in the grove as more of a spiritual rather than physical consumnation. And like the biblical OT pagans, these fantasy aliens do practice sexual rites at those high sacred places. It was predictable, and predicted.     Other reverses/parallels abound. The treasonous human science team almost directly parallels the ten faithless spies of biblical Moses's 12-man spy team, of whom only biblical Joshua(figured as an older Joshua by RDA Colonel Quaritch here) and Caleb proved strong and faithful (Colonel Quaritch to initially-zealous Sully: \\"I wish I had ten more like you\\"). Then there are the 12 RDA sectors which figuratively correspond to the biblical distribution of land among ancient Israel's twelve tribes. And there's marine (biblical Israelite) warrior Jake Sully as a traitorous refiguring of the biblical Caleb, except that Caleb's character is recast in the form a traitorous brother. It is yet another OT parallel from Deuteronomy, for the enemies of biblical Israel are sometimes described as \\"undercut\\" with crippled legs. Jake Sully's role as crippled traitor-to-be is strongly predictive of this, for in this reversed parallel fantasy, he gets to meet pagan Princess Neytiri (biblical Rahab) at Hometree (biblical Jericho), which eventually falls to the circling human army (biblical Walls and Trumpets assault).     In other details, alien wildlife are seen communicating with the indigenes (Balaam's biblically hilarious donkey). And oh, up there during the climactic battle is a naked and feathered Jake Sully with a crown of thorns sailing the sky on the back of a serpent-tailed red dragonbeast, holding at times a serpentine twist of hair-fiber and looking for all the world like your typical illustration of the biblical Canaanite gods Astaroth/Ashtoreth. Neopagans everywhere, rejoice!     As for the biblical Ark which brought down Jericho's walls on the seventh run after circling the city 6 times, figuratively substitute Shuttle 1-6 and its humongous daisycutter bomb as proxy for the presence and power of the biblical Hebrew deity (biblical YHWH-on-the-Ark). Watch as Cameron delightfully orchestrates the onscreen failure of the daisycutter/Ark to deliver the final providential blow, while the supposedly unaligned Eywa-mother-earth (Neytiri: \\"Our Great Mother does not take sides Jake\\") finally does the Abrahamic God Divine Intervention routine. It's pagan catnip,I tell you.    Cameron's counter-biblical SF fable even has some mocking humor. Go over the movie segment where high executive Parker (biblical high priest Eleazar) is looking down at imagery of Pandoran (biblical Canaan) sector 12 on the holotable. Yes, that's Twelve, which is metaphorical for the OT tribe of Benjamin from which the future hebrew messiah would descend. Parker then revealingly/mockingly comments, \\"Stop... Where .. Stop. Jesus... ...I mean, look at all that cheddar...\\" (Google hint: Cheesus). Marvelously allegorical mocking word-play! For the many post-modern/ post-Western/ post-Christian/ cryptomystics out there this is as good as it gets.    Avatar is full of parallels and strong metaphors, some not obvious yet others blindingly so given the narrative order and context. How to explain all that Cameron wrought, and why? Well, we know that Cameron has this thing about disputing biblical stories. We also know what he did say about his 2006 biblical-sort-of documentary The Exodus Decoded: \\"...Exodus is a story we all know. We were all raised with a biblical view of the world, which still has a resounding influence on Western culture...\\" \\"...Here, we've found evidence that may set the Exodus clock back 200 years. We can shove this film in the experts' faces and start a dialogue. History is often told by the victors. It is edited by subsequent rulers, who chisel away at it to show themselves in a better light. History is a moving target, and we should not be afraid to be provocative about it...\\" Tellingly, this contentious Discovery Channel whodunnit was his last major project prior to Avatar.    If today's neopagans could somehow revise the original OT story into a fantastical triumph for those old Canaanites in place of their historical defeat and conquest by the biblical Israelites, this movie is their gift. It seems Cameron is Speaker-for-the-Dead of all vanquished pagans, voicing ghostly wishes from beyond the grave for what could have been, but never was.    Excellent movie-making, though.

I was a little afraid that he was trying to spin out the franchise and would run out of good ideas, churning out more of the same. Instead, Riordan creates three new appealing characters, and puts a really unexpected spin on the whole Olympian world that caught me by surprise! I won't say more here because it would spoil the book, but I was suitably impressed that Riordan had the imagination to come up with such a cool idea. It gave me a new world to discover, instead of rehashing the same old. Good job Rick Riordan!    This time around, the characters are older (15-16 years old) and there is a sweet little romance going on between two of them; the original Percy Jackson books started with Percy being 12 years old, and there was no sappy stuff till almost the last book. I think young readers might like the original series better because Percy starts off closer to their age. I was also a little tired of the \\"The world is going to end!\\" theme. The original series started off with the lesser adventures of Percy Jackson and culminated with an apocalyptic war at the end--this book throws you right into yet another apocalyptic war. I was left wondering, \\"How many times does the world have to almost end?\\" Although I liked the new twist in the story, my husband did find the story lines repetitive and too similar to the original series. Younger readers may not be so picky.    I highly recommend this series to allreaders from young to adult. I'd read the entire series before reading \\"The Lost Hero\\" though, as it wouldn't make sense otherwise. I also think young readers would enjoy starting with the early Percy Jackson books first, as Percy is closer to their age.

B003L20ICE|5.0|Hollywood has been all a buzz with \\"The Kids Are All Right,\\" a new film out by director Lisa Cholodenko. This is the first major Hollywood film about a fairly common family, with one exception. The parents are both women.      \\"The Kids Are All Right,\\" stars female heavyweight actors Julianne Moore and Annette Bening as lesbian parents of two children whom have come to be curious about their natural father (only known as a sperm donor). The insertion of this character, played by Mark Ruffalo, causes the balance of the family to go askew, and perhaps damages it beyond repair.      But the story line is not what has all the movie reviewers worked up and excited. The performances so artfully crafted by Julianne Moore and Annette Bening are ones that will be studied by students of the craft. Lisa Cholodenko takes the characters and delicately peels away the layers of suburban convention and eco-pretentiousness to reveal the broken-hearted awareness of passion missing from a relationship. Moore and Bening are at their best, portraying long-loved partners that time, rearing children and careers have drained them of the love that once brought them together.      Bening especially shines as a woman with heavy responsibilities and fears that her manicured family unit may be changed forever. In one scene Bening sings the words from Joni Mitchell's \\"Blue\\" and we see a middle-aged woman with a heart exposed and for just a moment completely relinquishing the walls the protected her so well before.      Julianne Moore's performance is one more subtle and quiet. Yet, within this character that always put others before her own desires, it's apparent that a yearning to be seen and heard may break her away from a life that has been comfortable and easy.      The children played by Mia Wasikowska and Josh Hutcherson are well casted and the supporting role by beautiful Yaya DaCosta adds necessary zest to the story.      Obviously this film is not going to please many due to the lesbianstory line, but in the end it is a film more about families and love than it is about sexual orientation. See this film when you get the chance to, it's a promise that it will be in the mix around award season and you will want to know for yourself that \\"The Kids Are All Right.\\"    \\"The Kids Are All Right\\" is rated R for sexually explicit scenes and has a run time of 1 hr. and 46 minutes    For more film reviews and news from Kay Shackleton see: [...]    Kay Shackleton is a film historian with special focus on Silent Films, you can see her work at: [...]

Glenn was sick/in a fight and got his nose broken/having drug issues (or all of the above) just before the Seventh Star tour, and was in no shape vocally to perform. After that, Glen fell apart more each performance, and was replaced by Ray Gillen within days.  The live bonus tracks feature Ray instead of Glenn on vocals, and that's where things go wrong. Whether you're a Ray Gillen fan or not,these live versions are nothing like the Seventh Star studio tracks! The first (and best) live show that Glenn Hughes performed with Black Sabbath (Cleveland 3/21/86) should have been remastered and added as the bonus tracks... they are the only ones that compliment the Seventh Star release and would have truly let you hear and judge this unique \\"Black Sabbath\\" lineup live before it's brief time was up.

B0040JHXT8|5.0|I got the vinyl pressing of The Promise today.     It is dead quiet and absolutely flat. Very good sound quality - not great sonics, won't become your system test or show off record, but very good. And that is absolutely wonderful considering the track record for Springsteen on vinyl. It's a joy for me to be able to say that this sounds good.    And in terms of the music, no one (not even D.J.!) will be surprised at it's greatness. This is from the same well as Darkness. It isn't more Darkness, but its from the same waters.      There are a lot of great new 2010 albums, some even brilliant; but this is in a league of its own.     No one has done it before. Releasing a 21 song album that matches the most legendary work of their career and is literally from the era of that work. For me it's as incredible as if the Beatles had released more from the Abbey Road sessions, Dylan more Highway 61 or Bringin it All Back Home period recordings, Miles more of Kind of Blue. (Or even more Alvin and the Chipmunks \\"Chipmunk Punk)! This is the stuff of my dreams.    Yup, I said it matches. By that I did not intend to imply that it was more of the same...that it was \\"Darkness Part Two\\".  These songs and others were left off Darkness because Springsteen was aiming to create exactly what he released... the material was not left off on the whole because he felt it was of \\"lessor\\" quality, but because it did not fit the work he was aiming to create. In fact other members of the band balked at his dropping many of these songs because they felt they were just as good. Springsteen has never said that they weren't as good, only that they did not belong on \\"Darkness on the Edge of Town\\".      That does not mean that they are as powerful, or as dark, or even as poignant, although some of them are. It means that the musicians who played them felt they were also great Bruce Springsteen songs. As do I. If you don't hear them as such because they are not more Darkness On the Edge of Town per se,then you are missing on some great Springsteen music circa 1977. You are missing some of the REST of what he was thinking/creating at the time. I maintain my opinion that this is as exciting as the Beatles releasing more from the Abbey Road sessions would be.     The Promise is my unequivocal pick as vinyl release of the decade.

B00428ZRFE|3.0|Rihanna is one of pop music's most recognizable figures.  Rihanna has released five albums in an incredibly short span of time where most artists take years between successive albums. Keeping up with her fast pace and desire to stay `relevant,'  Rihanna follows her moderately successful [[ASIN:B002UXRH10 Rated R]] with a tamer, less flashy effort [[ASIN:B00428ZRFE Loud]]. 'LOUD' is by no means a `bomb,' but nor is it the unrequited triumph of her best effort [[ASIN:B0018S6YO4 Good Girl Gone Bad: Reloaded]] or her rebellious, captivating 'Rated R.'  This album is more on par with efforts [[ASIN:B0009XFJ0O Music of the Sun]] (2005) or [[ASIN:B000EQ4G5K A Girl Like Me]] (2006);  those albums had notable hits, but were easily eclipsed by 'Good Girl Gone Bad' and 'Rated R.'  'LOUD' is not absent of hits - that would be an overstatement considering that the exceptional \\"Only Girl (In the World)\\" has ascended to the top of the pop charts much quicker than \\"Russian Roulette\\" or \\"Hard\\" could ever hope to.  That said, there is no \\"Rude Boy\\" or \\"Umbrella\\" to truly make this a well rounded, Rihanna effort; it is a mixed-bag.<br /><br />\\"S&M\\" opens the album strongly with its euro-pop driven production work.  While the trend is growing a bit old at this point (no offense to David Guetta), this cut works for Rihanna.  It is, less captivating than the incredible \\"Wait Your Turn\\" from Rated R.  That said, the songwriting is catchy and the production work solid.  The track is overt (\\"Cause I may be bad, but I'm perfectly good at it/sex in the air, I don't care, I like the smell of it...\\"), if in a subtler way than say \\"Rude Boy.\\" \\"What's My Name\\" featuring ubiquitous rapper/singer Drake works well (it is now another #1 hit for Rihanna), though it does not dare eclipse Rihanna's finer material.  Drake's appearance is good here, but it doesn't stand out - maybe it is because his guest spot is at the beginning of the track and so brief.  The track grows the slightest bit repetitive given all the gimmicky and continuous production loop, but again it is not a bad listen.<br /><br />\\"Cheers Drink To That\\" is an oddity, finding Rihanna exploiting the lower range of her voice.  The vocal performance turns out to be somewhat monotone until the hook, where Rihanna sings: \\"cheers to the freak-n weekend, I drink to that...\\"  The most interesting moment may be the breakdown section, where the production is stripped and contrasts a rather repetitive cut.  \\"Fading\\" is a better cut, using piano within the production and making the dance-pop artist more vocally-viable here.  \\"Only Girl In the World\\" is the valedictory performance, placing the pop artist against an overt dance-friendly beat.  The songwriting stands out (Well as far as being catchy): \\"Want you to make me feel like I'm the only girl in the world, like I'm the only one that you'll ever love, like I'm the only one who knows your heart...\\"<br /><br />\\"California King Bed\\" is somewhat a miss and ill-fitting, but does provide a change of pace from typical Rihanna fare.  Here, Rihanna's bare vocals shine initially given the stripped production work.  Somewhat reminiscent of the fine \\"Firebomb\\" from Rated R,  the track fails in comparison to that cut and ends up being a bit `too manic' and `melodramatic.'  \\"Man Down\\" is even worse, finding Rihanna rehashing her tropical roots with this sub-par cut.  It might be a different affair if \\"Man Down\\" felt like top-notch, first-rate Rihanna, but it suffers from being a bore to listen to and frankly all over the place.  \\"Raining Men\\" featuring ubiquitous female rapper Nicki Minaj helps to redirect the falling momentum of the album with a more hip-hop driven cut that would have been perfectly at home on a Beyonc&eacute; effort.  It isn't perfect (and a bit busy), but it is superior to former cuts \\"California King Bed\\" and \\"Man Down.\\"<br /><br />\\"Complicated\\" features captivating production work, finding the track building up gradually.  \\"Skin\\" is the sole track that feels completely different from past Rihanna cuts, though it ends up lasting the slightest bit too long in duration.  That said, it is one of the better cuts at the `tail-end' of the album.  \\"Love the Way You Lie, Pt. II\\" featuring Eminem serves to the same effect as Alicia Keys' \\"Empire State of Mind (Broken Down)\\" from 'The Element of Freedom' - a path already tread by its parent artist.<br /><br />Essentially, many had guessed that 'LOUD' would be more of an `anachronism' than a forward-thinking effort from its muse.  I am not surprised given first two singles \\"Only Girl in the World\\" and \\"What's My Name\\" are tamer than recent singles and not nearly as innovative. The musicians and instruments vary from song to song on this album, but you do get some great background vocals on most songs.    As some of the other reviewers have stated, this album reflects a more mature, Kid Rock. Plus, The Rolling Stone magazine review of this album (Nov.25,2010 #1118), states \\"it has the Skynyrd guitar attack, the Leon Russell-style gospel backup singers, and some fire down-below boogie from Detroit's Bob Seger\\". I agree with their assessment. However, what makes it different, is that the Kid is free from genre barriers, like those 70's songs, and it incorporates some Country, Soul, Blues, Rap, and also a little Gospel, along with the 70's style Rock.    The song \\"Born Free\\" is a statement of the Freedom he has found in life and in music. The song \\"Care\\", with \\"Country Queen\\", Martina McBride & \\"Atlanta Rapper\\" T.I., is a perfect example of how the \\"Kid\\", can mix genres, and make it work. \\"Care\\" sings about things that you can't do, but at least you can \\"Care\\" ...\\"it's what you can do...\\", so Raps T.I. Martina really pours her country soul into this one also. Kid & Sheryl had the big hit \\"Picture\\", from a few years back. They sing good together. \\"Collide\\" is a great song about taking chances (and being free). \\"Flyin' High\\" is a great \\"feel good\\" song, featuring Zac Brown, from Atlanta. Kid was on Zac's \\"Pass the Jar\\" CD/DVD. If you like Kid Rock, you would really enjoy his performance of some Southern Rock on \\"Pass the Jar\\". The rest of the songs are good solid \\"sing along\\" songs, reflecting the economic times we are in, such as \\"When It Rains\\", and \\"Times Like These\\". \\"Rock Bottom Blues\\" is a bluesy one, but it is also a statement, about how you can still have good times anyway...\\"it feels good to me...\\" sings Kid Rock. It contains some great harmonia music. It's suppose to have Trace Adkins singing background vocals, but I can't hear him. \\"For the First Time in a Long Time\\", it's kinda like a song of reflections on where the Kid's come from and the success he has achieved. \\"For the first time, in a long time\\", he's feeling good about himself.    The CD has a hidden 13th track, of a remix of \\"Care\\". It contains some very, very, Soulful vocals, and T.I. contributes some great Gospel, Soulful, Rap. It makes you want to \\"Care\\". Again, this is a terrific song, with mixed genres.    Overall, this is a good solid album, and I think it will go down in history as one of Kid's best. Thanks!!!

0618918248|5.0|This is the one, the ultimate book to have for any atheist/agnostic/freethinker. Renowned scientist Richard Dawkins uses reason, logic and scientific evidence to debunk the notion that any deity exists: whether personal or impersonal. He also highlights the overwhelming lack of supernatural evidence for said deities. Add to all that the dangers associated with blind faith (slavery, 'holy wars', racism, sexism, etc...) and Dawkins effectively argues in favor of abandoning superstition in favor of sound reason, thus making this book essentially the \\"Freethinker's Bible\\". The only problem one might find with \\"The God Delusion\\" is the ruthless style with which Dawkins writes. I could see how such a stubborn stance by an atheist might turn off an equally stubborn fundamentalist believer who might otherwise be convinced to open his or her mind based on the evidence provided. Other than that, however, this book is a breath of fresh air (and reason) in a world suffocated by radical and irrational dogma. Overall, well worth the price.

I also enjoyed the minor differences in Swedish versus American culture, most notably around the Swedes liberal attitude toward sex.  A lot of American pop fiction writers use heavy, almost awkward sexual scenes in their work, whereas Larsson sprinkles sex lightly about with no more meaning than having a cup of coffee. It was actually quite refreshing.<br /><br />A review is less useful without some criticism, so here goes:  As the complex mystery and associated crime began to unfold, I felt like Larsson went over the top and made what was a believe story (sort-of) into a very far fetched one.  To me, it was already a great mystery; he could have toned it down and still had the desired effect.  I am being vague intentionally for those who haven't read the book yet.<br /><br />All in all, I can enthusiastically recommend The Girl With the Dragon Tattoo; just be prepared to suspend your disbelief in a few sections.

B003XRDYX2|4.0|Bob Dylan is the king of bootlegs. Even before he started officially releasing his catalog, his fans were collecting and trading his unreleased out-takes and concerts.<br /><br />The latest release of The Original Mono Recordings is largely a waste of time, unless you don't already own these CDs or albums.<br /><br />Collectors and completists will want the whole package and may find this review offensive.<br /><br />There is nothing new here when compared with with earlier official bootlegs such as  Tell Tale Signs, Basement Tapes or The Bootleg Series, Vols. 1-3 : Rare And Unreleased, 1961-1991.<br /><br />I'm probably the only reviewer who listened to all nine CDs, the original vinyl and the SACD releases to compare the differences, which is why it took 3 weeks to prepare the review.<br /><br />You only only listen to so much Dylan in a given day. I've played this set over and over to figure out what was of value.<br /><br />What you get in the package: Bob Dylan The Original Mono Recording, cute packaging with tiny print; the release of Bob Dylan's first 8 LPs includes: 9 CDs, a free digital download, booklet by Greil Marcus, bonus CD of  Dylan's 1963 Brandeis University concert, in a hardboard slip case.<br /><br />The cases are miniature versions of the original LP covers. That's cute but you can't hold them in your hand and read the liner notes so what's the point?<br /><br />On the SACD release, the covers are three folds that allow the liner notes to be legibly printed or include a booklet.<br /><br />Compared with holding the original albums in your hand while listening, this set is low on creating 60s nostalgia.<br /><br />The booklet with pictures and notes by Greil Marcus is another exercise on miniaturization and perhaps the only reason to purchase the The Original Mono Recordings (Vinyl) at a whopping $205.<br /><br />The digital download in high quality 320 Kpbs mp3 format was a bonus if the set was ordered directly from Sony. I didn't expect to get this from Amazon.com.<br /><br />The download comes compressed in an 890 MB file which easily extracted in one sub-directory. It was simple work to make 8 sub-directories for each LP and move the files.<br /><br />In reality, Windows Media Player or a variety of programs can make mp3s or WAV rips of the CDs once you own them, which is  legal in Canada.<br /><br />So what's the big deal about \\"Original Mono\\" versions? Are they better, more like the original?<br /><br />Greil Marcus waxes eloquent about listening to Bob Dylan through one speaker up in our bedrooms back in the 1960s, in the accompanying booklet to this package.<br /><br />It is true that most people heard Bob Dylan in mono back in the 1960s. However, some of us had stereo and the difference is not worth spending $100 to hear.<br /><br />It's like the overblown production of The Beatles Mono last year. Everyone raved about them in reviews and we all listened to the stereo versions if we wanted to hear the music. Is this a gimmick we will have to endure each fall as the greats of rock and roll re-issue their catalogs in mono?<br /><br />In reality, from Bob Dylan to Another Side of Bob Dylan are mono - the man and his guitar.<br /><br />From Bringing it All Back Home - the break out rock and roll album - to John Wesley Harding the CDs should be listened to in stereo. The Mono versions squeeze the instruments into one speaker which destroys definition.<br /><br />When people rave on about the great sound that was recorded in the 1960s get them to give their heads a collective shake.  The recording back then was primitive with tape noise, lack of detail and distortion.<br /><br />The Original Mono recordings can't make a silk purse out of a sow's ear. If you listen closely to Bringing It All Back Home there is plenty of 1960s distortion.<br /><br />The Original Mono are not as clear as the SACD Bob Dylan - Limited Edition Catalog Box Set. Like most modern CDs, the bass is pumped up at the expense of detail. On the original vinyl LPs, the bass sounds real with actual notes, dynamics and overtones. On the Original Mono it's more like a louder \\"thump, thump, thump\\".<br /><br />Bob Dylan's first 8 albums<br /><br />There are millions of Dylan fans who don't have the originals for whom this collection will be a revelation. Since I've been following Dylan since he hit the folk scene 1961, these albums are old friends.<br /><br />From Bob Dylan to John Wesley Harding is a span of  5 years in which Bob Dylan changed folk music and rock and roll with the prodigious output of 83 songs composed and recorded.  Most of the songs are classics such as \\"Blowin in the Wind\\", \\"Like A Rolling Stone\\", \\"All Along The Watchtower,\\" Don't Think Twice It's All Right. These are songs that have been covered by everyone and become part of our lives.<br /><br />Bob Dylan songs were meant to be sung by everyone, and everyone has obliged Dylan. They are songs of protest, of love, love gone wrong, get even songs and have a good time songs. They are simple and complex, weird lyrics that spin off into infinity and lyrics grounded in the feeling of seeing someone you love coming along the beach.<br /><br />After Bob Dylan, singers had to get busy and write their own songs. Many tried to follow his lead but few made the transition from corny tin-pan-alley to the hip world.
